    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27495777?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27495777" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10624597148080062719"><b>Purgatorio = Purgatory (The Divine Comedy, #2), Dante Alighieri</b><br/><br/>Purgatory is the second part of Dante's Divine Comedy, following the Inferno, and preceding the Paradiso. <br/><br/>The poem was written in the early 14th century. It is an allegory telling of the climb of Dante up the Mount of Purgatory, guided by the Roman poet Virgil, except for the last four cantos at which point Beatrice takes over as Dante's guide.<br/><br/>Having survived the depths of Hell, Dante and Virgil ascend out of the undergloom to the Mo</span>
  <span id="freeText10624597148080062719" style="display:none"><b>Purgatorio = Purgatory (The Divine Comedy, #2), Dante Alighieri</b><br /><br />Purgatory is the second part of Dante's Divine Comedy, following the Inferno, and preceding the Paradiso. <br /><br />The poem was written in the early 14th century. It is an allegory telling of the climb of Dante up the Mount of Purgatory, guided by the Roman poet Virgil, except for the last four cantos at which point Beatrice takes over as Dante's guide.<br /><br />Having survived the depths of Hell, Dante and Virgil ascend out of the undergloom to the Mountain of Purgatory on the far side of the world. <br /><br />The Mountain is on an island, the only land in the Southern Hemisphere, created by the displacement of rock which resulted when Satan's fall created Hell (which Dante portrays as existing underneath Jerusalem). <br /><br />The mountain has seven terraces, corresponding to the seven deadly sins or "seven roots of sinfulness." The classification of sin here is more psychological than that of the Inferno, being based on motives, rather than actions. <br /><br />It is also drawn primarily from Christian theology, rather than from classical sources. However, Dante's illustrative examples of sin and virtue draw on classical sources as well as on the Bible and on contemporary events. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش نسخه فارسی: سال 1968میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: برزخ؛ نویسنده: دانته آلیگیری؛ برگردان: شجاع الدین شفا؛ تهران، امیرکبیر، 1335؛ چاپ دیگر 1347؛ جلد دوم از سه جلد؛ چاپ ششم 1378؛ شابک جلد دوم 9640003999؛ چاپ بیست و یکم 1393؛ جلد نخست: دوزخ؛ جلد دوم برزخ؛ جلد سوم: بهشت؛ ترجمه از زبان ایتالیائی؛ موضوع شعر شاعران ایتالیائی - سده 14م<br /><br />عقاب سمبل برزخ، مظهر صعود به طرف آسمان و خورشید<br /><br />سرود اول برزخ<br />اکنون زورق اندیشه ی من، که دریایی چنین آشفته را در پشت سر نهاده، بادبان برداشته است، تا در روی امواجی نکوتر به راه خود رود؛ و اینک من، در باره ی این قلمرو دومین، نغمه، ساز خواهم کرد؛ که در آن روح آدمی تصفیه میشود، و شایستگی صعود به آسمان را پیدا میکند؛ ای پریان مقدس سرود، مرده را بگوئید تا زندگی از سر گیرد؛ زیرا اینک من در اختیار شمایم، و «کالیوپه» را بگویید که دمی، روی در اینجا بنماید؛ تا نغمه ی مرا با نوای خوش خویش که «پیکا»های نگونبخت جاذبه اش را احساس کرده، امید بخشایش را از کف داده اند، همراهی کند<br /><br />کالیوپه - بزرگترین پری از پریان الهام بخش نه گانه، که بر سرایندگان اشعار حماسی سرپرستی میکرد<br /><br />پیکا - دختران نه گانه ی «پیروس»، پادشاه «مقدونیه»، که همگی آوازی خوش داشتند، و ادعا میکردند از پریان الهامبخش نیز بهتر آواز میخوانند، «کالیوپه» با آواز خود آنها را مغلوب کرد، در نتیجه این دختران طبق توافق خود با «کالیوپه»، به دست «آپولون» خدای موسیقی و هنر، به گنجشک تبدیل شدند<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 11/04/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="10624597148080062719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer12856298698872416484">This is a great book, but does require the notes to make any sense of it. Hundreds of characters from Danté's Italy not to mention host of mythological and Biblical ones tended to distract me just reading the poetry and appreciating Dante's wonderful descriptions. I have to stop at the end of each chapter and read the notes to understand the context and people that Dante is referring to. I agree that it would be impossible to ever write this book without references to contemporary politics and s</span>
  <span id="freeText12856298698872416484" style="display:none">This is a great book, but does require the notes to make any sense of it. Hundreds of characters from Danté's Italy not to mention host of mythological and Biblical ones tended to distract me just reading the poetry and appreciating Dante's wonderful descriptions. I have to stop at the end of each chapter and read the notes to understand the context and people that Dante is referring to. I agree that it would be impossible to ever write this book without references to contemporary politics and so it probably felt dated in that manner even as Boccacio was writing Dante's biography and promoting this up-to-then banned masterpiece 50 years after Dante finished it. Perhaps a lot of postmodern literature will suffer from the same feeling of obsolescence? Am I a hypocrite because I also adore Pynchon, DFW, Proust and Joyce who also require dense footnotes to completely understand? Perhaps but my point was more that I have to read this in a stop and start manner and it takes a little away from the pleasure of reading straight through. <br /><br />All that being said, there is nothing in Western literature quite like the Divine Comedy and this central volume of 33 cantos is of unsurpassed depth and beauty.<br /><br />If I were to express this book in a picture, I would probably see something like this: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.comuseum.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/dong-qichang_shady-trees-in-a-summer-landscape.jpg">http://www.comuseum.com/wp-content/up...</a> from Dong Qichang (董其昌, 1555-1636) as it seems to express the long hazardous climb and yet the peaceful conclusion awaiting Dante at the top.</span>

<div id="review_2424510229" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2424510229" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2424510229?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486815336" href="/book/show/32201954-purgatorio">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=european-lit">european-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2424510229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12754198897434525568">There is nothing much to add after the second read. I was quite surprised and happy to find that I have understood the work to a considerable degree in my first read. The only thing I want to add here is that I haven't appreciated enough the picturesque quality the Cantos bring to light, the vivid imagination they create in your mind. It was a fascinating experience. I would still say it is less imaginative than the Inferno, but only in comparison. As a stand-alone work, it has all the wonders t</span>
  <span id="freeText12754198897434525568" style="display:none">There is nothing much to add after the second read. I was quite surprised and happy to find that I have understood the work to a considerable degree in my first read. The only thing I want to add here is that I haven't appreciated enough the picturesque quality the Cantos bring to light, the vivid imagination they create in your mind. It was a fascinating experience. I would still say it is less imaginative than the Inferno, but only in comparison. As a stand-alone work, it has all the wonders to draw in the readers. Quite honestly, after this reread, I'm beginning to appreciate the quality of this work and to fully understand why <i> The Divine Comedy </i> has been regarded as a masterpiece all through these centuries.<br /><br />***********************************************<br /><br />The Purgatorio is the second part of Dante's masterpiece, the Divine Comedy. This is where according to Dante the sinners purge their sins by being repentant of the sins they have committed on earth. Unlike in Inferno, purgatory deals with sins more psychological than physical with many tiers (or "terraces" as they are called) punishing the sins committed through the mind rather than by the body. <br /><br />Purgatorio too is influenced by Christian theology, mythology, philosophy, literature and Dante's own political and religious views. But on a comparative note with Inferno, one can sense that more of theology and Dante's views on the relationship of God, Church, and State taking shape in the beautiful verses. Dante chooses his sinners from real life and mythology as in Inferno. Interestingly, Dante meets another guide here - Statius, the Roman poet, who team with Virgil helps him through his journey until finally, Dante meets “she who makes his journey from hell to paradise possible” - Beatrice. <br /><br />The same lyricism and the beautiful metaphors that I have come to love in Inferno are preserved here as well, and that makes this poetic exercise an unforgettable one. However, if I'm to compare Purgatorio with Inferno, I have to admit, Purgatorio lacks the imagination that encompassed Inferno. But as a stand-alone, Purgatorio has its own beauty and can be enjoyed for its own merit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12754198897434525568" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1643998178" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1643998178" />
    <a title="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8930751-arkha-os-rk"><img alt="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592525719p2/8930751.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1643998178?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)" href="/user/show/8930751-arkha-os-rk">ἀρχαῖος (arkhaîos)(RK)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780520045163" href="/book/show/1318422.Purgatorio">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8930751-arkha-os-rk?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8930751-arkha-os-rk?shelf=witches-gods-other-imaginaries">witches-gods-other-imaginaries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8930751-arkha-os-rk?shelf=medieval">medieval</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8930751-arkha-os-rk?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8930751-arkha-os-rk?shelf=italy">italy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1643998178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1499770149817669795">In The Inferno, Dante used his many skills of philosophical and theological argument, poetry, knowledge of the classics and the Christian Church to both show his readers the punishments that await them if they do not change their ways, but, also, to carry forth his own political and personal polemics. <br/><br/>Here, in Purgatorio, he shows himself to be of even greater genius. I was expecting to encounter those who, despite their good intentions, have found themselves suffering great penance for their si</span>
  <span id="freeText1499770149817669795" style="display:none">In The Inferno, Dante used his many skills of philosophical and theological argument, poetry, knowledge of the classics and the Christian Church to both show his readers the punishments that await them if they do not change their ways, but, also, to carry forth his own political and personal polemics. <br /><br />Here, in Purgatorio, he shows himself to be of even greater genius. I was expecting to encounter those who, despite their good intentions, have found themselves suffering great penance for their sins. They do so willingly in the knowledge that the time will come when they will be granted absolution and be welcomed into Paradise. <br /><br />Again, Dante reveals his genius in intermingling Classical and Christian myth to create forceful stories of sin and redemption. One need not be a Christian to feel the force of the arguments. <br /><br />The true genius of the work only becomes clear in Canto XXXI and beyond when Dante finally encounters Beatrice. Not just another pretty face, Beatrice in all her purity is both Dante's protector and his judge. Her initial scorn for him makes the reader wonder why she has gone to so much trouble to bring him to the edge of Paradise. He is such a lowly worm. <br /><br />Dante's extensive knowledge, his poetic genius, his commitment to his message and his ability to combine and invent all bring too me a certain humbleness that most writers can never bring to the fore. It seems that Dante has gone beyond the merely human in order to put it all together. <br /><br />Now on to Paradiso.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1499770149817669795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38773233" class="readable"

  <span id="freeText17774118259901733520" style="display:none"><b>For <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/277496?chapter=4" rel="nofollow noopener">the Celebrity Death Match Review Tournament</a>, <i>Les Trois Mousquetaires</i> (31) versus <i>The Divine Comedy</i> (26)</b><br /><br />- Welcome to Purgatory. Name, please?<br /><br />- Ah, D'Artagnan. I think there might have been some kind of...<br /><br />- We'll deal with that in a moment. Could we just start by taking care of the Deadly Sins paperwork? <br /><br />- Um...<br /><br />- Thank you. Number one, Pride. Any offences?<br /><br />- Look, obviously I'm pretty damn cool, but, you know...<br /><br />- Pride, tick. Please pick up a stone on your way out, I think you'll want an L. Number two, Envy...<br /><br />- Can we speed this up a bit? I've got about two hundred and eighteen counts of Wrath but a clean slate on Envy, Sloth, Covetousness and Gluttony.<br /><br />- Wait a minute... cross, tick, cross, cross, cross. I must say, I wish everyone would be this cooperative. Oops! One left. How are you on Lust?<br /><br />- Um, let me see, I suppose I shouldn't really have raped Ketty. But, well...<br /><br />- She said no but she meant yes? <br /><br />- What I was going to say was that I only did it to get close to Milady. She's the one I was actually after. <br /><br />- Let me check the records. You raped Milady's maid so you could impersonate her lover and get into her bed? I'm afraid...<br /><br />- But look! It's not as bad as it sounds. I was only doing <i>that</i> to save Constance, there was no other way. She'd been kidnapped and I was afraid she'd be killed...<br /><br />- Constance was your wife?<br /><br />- Ah, in fact she was married to someone else. But I really loved her, so...<br /><br />- Anyway, the important thing was that you saved her life?<br /><br />- Look, I did my best, right? But that psycho bitch Milady went and poisoned her. It wasn't my fault...<br /><br />- Excuse me. Monsieur D'Artagnan, I must ask you to consider carefully. This place is all about sincere repentance. Are you completely sure you'd be happy here in Purgatory? <br /><br />- What do you mean? Of course I want to do it. My heart is pure.<br /><br />- Let me check again. Well, the thing I like about this job is that you never get a dull day. You're right.<br /><br />- So I can come in?<br /><br />- Sure. But make that stone an XL. No, an XXL.<br /><br />- Deal! Thank you.<br /><br />- You're welcome. Next please?<br /><br />- Ah, my name's Richelieu. Listen, I can explain everything...<br /><br /><b>Match point: <i>Les Trois Mousquetaires</i></b></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_884288491" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/884288491" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/884288491?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451528025" href="/book/show/208471.The_Purgatorio">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer884288491" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11804010748675570343"><br/><i>For better waters now the little bark<br/>of my indwelling powers raises her sails,<br/>and leaves behind that sea so cruel and dark.<br/><br/>Now shall I sing that second kingdom given<br/>the soul of man wherein to purge its guilt<br/>and so grow worthy to ascend to Heaven. </i><br/><br/>If the arhitecture of Inferno was a giant funnel with ever receding terraces hosting the souls of the eternally damned in a carefully orchestrated arrangement of crime and its alloted punishment, Purgatory turns out to be its mirror image above ground: </span>
  <span id="freeText11804010748675570343" style="display:none"><br /><i>For better waters now the little bark<br />of my indwelling powers raises her sails,<br />and leaves behind that sea so cruel and dark.<br /><br />Now shall I sing that second kingdom given<br />the soul of man wherein to purge its guilt<br />and so grow worthy to ascend to Heaven. </i><br /><br />If the arhitecture of Inferno was a giant funnel with ever receding terraces hosting the souls of the eternally damned in a carefully orchestrated arrangement of crime and its alloted punishment, Purgatory turns out to be its mirror image above ground: starting from sea level, Dante and his guide will have to climb a giant mountain constructed of succesive corniches where the souls seeking absolution for their sins endure the just sentences handed down from the higher authority of Heaven. The grand vision of the Renascentist poet that was only hinted at in the first book, is easier to follow now, as the allegoric parade of saints and sinners mirrors the movements of the demons and the damned below for almost every Canto. The personal quest of Dante to regain the path he lost at the beginning of the poem in the dark wood is again set against the background of the internecine wars between the city states of the Italian Peninsula, reiterating the major themes expressed down in Inferno: the separation of the spiritual from the secular power, the link between the classical world of Greco-Roman culture and Christianity, free will and predestination, the limits of Reason in providing answers to the spiritual needs of the soul.<br /><br /><i> He is insane who dreams that he may learn<br />by mortal reasoning the boundless orbit<br />Three Persons in One Substance fill and turn. </i><br /><br />The Cantos, and the Corniches of the Purgatory mountain, are organized by putting in balance seven sins with seven virtues. So, the first major difference between Inferno and Purgatory is that the lessons to be learned here are no longer about sinners being punished, but about how those acknowledging and willing to atone for past mistakes are shown the way to redeem their souls. The virtues reflect what I said earlier about the roots of the poem in both the Classical and the Christian etics, being grouped as three Sacred Virtues (Faith, Hope and Charity) and four Cardinal Virtues (Justice, Prudence, Temperance and Fortitude). What is particular about Dante’s interpretation of Sin, is the gradation of severity and the judgement of vice as a manifestation of love. <br /><br /><i> Natural love may never fall to error.<br />The other may, by striving to bad ends,<br />or by too little, or by too much fervor. </i><br /><br />Natural love being considered here the final aspiration and road to salvation for every human being, received as a gift from God at birth by everyone and misplaced along the way by:<br />-	the proud, the envious, the wrathful as examples of bad love;<br />-	the slothful as examples of too little love;<br />-	the avaricious, the gluttons, the lustful as examples of immoderate love.<br /><br /><i> Ah, what a difference between these trails<br />and those of Hell: here every entrance fills<br />with joyous song, and there with savage wails! </i><br /><br />If Dante is guided and encouraged along the way by the power of Reason illustrated by the spirit of Virgil assisted now by another classical poet named Statius, my support in decoding the text came from the commentaries of John Ciardi and the erudite posts and illustrations by fellow Goodreaders in the Commedia group. In all honesty, without this help the second part of the poem would have been a lot more difficult to enjoy than the first. In the example of the quote above, every step up the mountain is celebrated by a different hymn. I am unfamiliar with the traditions of the Catholic service, but professor MacAllister has this to say about how Dante plans carefully every line of his major opus and leaves nothing to chance:<br /><i> The whole Purgatorio is built upon the structure of a Mass. The Mass moreover is happening not on the mountain but in church with Dante devoutly following its well known steps. </i><br /><br />The allegory is operating on multiple levels, from one Canto to another. One of these levels is the battle between darkness and light, with the Inferno all darkness and the Paradiso all light, making the Purgatory a mixture of the two in its alteration of day and night. As Dante struggles up the mountain another level of allegory comes into play, dealing with gravity as the human soul is burdened at the beginning of the journey with the weight of its sins and worldy preoccupations, shedding them one by one as he witnesses the various examples of the punishment for vice and the rewards of virtues, to arrive at the gates of the Earthly Paradise almost weightless, carried away by its spiritual fervor. As Ciardi remarks :<br /><br /><i> There is suffering in Purgatory but no torment. The torment of the damned is endless, produces no change in the soul that endures it, and is imposed from without. The suffering of the souls in Purgatory, on the other hand, is temporary, is a means of purification, and is eagerly embraced as an act of the soul’s own will. </i><br /><br />The text of Purgatory was for me more convoluted and more ambitious than the one in Inferno, requiring more attention to detail and more frequent referrals to commentaries to decypher the frankly obscure passages where Dante shows off his geography and astronomy interests, transforming some of the stanzas into veritable puzzles – instead of saying ‘the Sun has just risen’, or ‘this guy comes from Padua’ he embarks on long , intricate allusions to past events, myths and legends or local rivers, mountains and villages that are completely unfamiliar to the modern reader. Other passages are brilliant in their analysis and exposition, with my favorite probably being the lengthy debate about free will between Dante, Virgil and Statius. I can truly understand how not only historians and poets, but also philosophers and sociologists can choose to study the Commedia in depth over long years. In the context of the period when it was written, I find it amazingly relevant still to the search for meaning and purpose of the individual in the modern world, in the study of the relation between the secular and the spiritual authority, in the passion for knowledge and for preserving the treasures of ancient wisdom:<br /><br /><i> Mankind sees in the heavens alone the source<br />of all things, good and evil; as if by Law<br />they shaped all mortal actions in their course.<br /><br />If that were truly so, then all Free Will<br />would be destroyed, and there would be no justice<br />in giving bliss for virtue, pain for evil. </i><br /><br />also : <i> With reason as the principle of conduct, the soul is then responsible for its actions. </i><br /><br />I must admit than not all the arguments presented by Dante found resonance with my more atheistic world view. In the case of free will, Dante ultimately argues for abandoning the gift of Reason in favor of attaining Epiphany through unquestioning Faith. At the end of the ascent, Virgil congratulates Dante on conquering all doubts and logically choosing the correct path to salvation, but Virgil as a guide stops at the gates of the Terrestrial Paradise and passes the poet into the hands of Beatrice, as the incarnation of Divine Love.<br /><br /><i> Here your will is upright, free, and whole,<br />whatever your own impulse prompts you to:<br />lord of yourself I crown and mitre you. </i><br /><br />The finale of Purgatorio is as beautiful and spectacular as it is confusing, with a magnificent pageant of angels, saints, mythological beasts and allegorical processions, all preparing the way for the next and last part of the trilogy, for leaving the Earth behind and travelling to the nine celestial spheres of the Paradiso, guided this time by Beatrice.<br /><br />I had a few more quotes I wanted to use in my review, but it’s already early in the morning, and I have to leave for work at 6. I’ll just leave them here, as another example of the numerous avenues of debate and of the rare gems to be found in the text:<br /><br />On the wars between brothers in the peninsula, with reference to the two families made famous later by Shakespeare (Montagues and Capulets):<br /><br /><i> O wretched land, search all your coasts, your seas,<br />the bosom of your hills – where will you find a single part <br />that knows the joy of peace? </i><br /><br />On the nascent Renaissance spirit of curiosity and faith in the power of Reason:<br /><br /><i> My eyes, always intent to look ahead<br />to some new thing, finding delight in learning,<br />lost little time in doing as he said. </i><br /><br />On the degradation of Papal authority:<br /><br /><i> ... since the Church has thought to be<br />two governments at once, she sinks in muck,<br />befouling both her power and her ministry. </i><br /><br />On the inspiration Dante found in the works of the great poets of antiquity:<br /><br /><i> The sparks that were my seeds of passion came<br />from the celestial fire which has enkindled<br />more than a thousand poets; I mean the flame<br /><br />of the Aeneid, the mother that brought forth,<br />the nurse that gave suck to my song. Without it<br />I could not have weighed half a penny’s worth. </i><br /><br />On the thirst for knowledge and for spiritual salvation:<br /><br /><i> This is the day your hungry soul shall be<br />fed on the golden apples men have sought<br />on many different boughs so ardently. </i><br /><br />On the artist need and passion to express his feelings:<br /><br /><i> When Love inspires me with delight,<br />or pain, or longing, I take careful note,<br />and as he dictates in my soul, I write. </i><br /><br />and, finally, a stanza that I find expresses my gratitude and respect for the achievement of this literary titan:<br /><br /><i> ... in my heart a grateful place to feast his name was laid. </i><br /><br />Beam me up, Beatrice! <br />I’m ready for the next stage of the journey!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11804010748675570343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_527177888" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/527177888" />
    <a title="David Lafferty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7166145-david-lafferty"><img alt="David Lafferty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516462978p2/7166145.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/527177888?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lafferty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lafferty" href="/user/show/7166145-david-lafferty">David Lafferty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444421" href="/book/show/19173.The_Divine_Comedy_Vol_2">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer527177888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13156376867520022589"><i> "…like people going to visit a great city like Paris and only spending a few days in the sewers…"</i> ...Dorothy Sayers on only reading Inferno and stopping there<br/><br/>Purgatorio is my favorite book of the Divine Comedy. While Inferno is the most popular and arguably the most accessible to the new reader of Dante, it ultimately is a book of despair and hopelessness. I spent a couple of years immersed in Inferno while writing a book on the poem and discovered it was beginning to have a subtle depressing e</span>
  <span id="freeText13156376867520022589" style="display:none"><i> "…like people going to visit a great city like Paris and only spending a few days in the sewers…"</i> ...Dorothy Sayers on only reading Inferno and stopping there<br /><br />Purgatorio is my favorite book of the Divine Comedy. While Inferno is the most popular and arguably the most accessible to the new reader of Dante, it ultimately is a book of despair and hopelessness. I spent a couple of years immersed in Inferno while writing a book on the poem and discovered it was beginning to have a subtle depressing effect on me. Certainly the ghoulies, gore and creative punishments are brilliant literary elements, but it's still a world of sadness, terror, and grief. In additon, it's important to keep in mind that Inferno is only one third (plus one additional canto) of Dante's poem. <br /><br />Without mentioning any spoilers, the poem begins with Dante washing off the filth of Hell. I felt a similar cleansing as a reader. Unlike Inferno, this is a book of hope and redemption (all souls in Purgatory are destined for Heaven). Although not quite as accessible as Inferno, Purgatory is still very readable and understandable. As in all of the Commedia, the poem operates on multiple levels and demands a lifetime of readings.<br /><br />Mark Musa is a wonderful translator. Since the Commedia is written in Italian with a complex rhyme scheme, translating to English is more art than science. Rather than try to match the rhyme scheme in vowel-poor English, or stick with straight prose, Musa strikes a nice balance using blank verse (unrhymed Iambic Pentameter). It still has the feel of the poem without sounding forced and preserves the meaning nicely. His notes are great for newbies to Dante, not too deep but enough to get you started.<br /><br />If you've made it through Inferno, you must read Purgatorio. Get out of the sewers!<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13156376867520022589" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3316578358" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3316578358" />
    <a title="Daniel Chaikin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18695911-daniel-chaikin"><img alt="Daniel Chaikin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534027159p2/18695911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3316578358?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Chaikin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Chaikin" href="/user/show/18695911-daniel-chaikin">Daniel Chaikin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385497008" href="/book/show/208472.Purgatorio">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3316578358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14598282198733567901">I read this in such a pleasant way, every morning reading the Hollander summary, then the Canto itself, then going through the Hollander notes while reading the Canto a second time. There was ritual aspect to it. Then I finished and had nothing I felt I needed to say. This is and was so strange it's stumped my ability to write anything at all about this. So below is more a report than a review. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595113792i/29838316._SX540_.jpg' width='1000' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><i>Purgatorio</i> is nothing like <i>Inferno</i> in its impact. Here nothing is permanent, and the tragic aspects ar</span>
  <span id="freeText14598282198733567901" style="display:none">I read this in such a pleasant way, every morning reading the Hollander summary, then the Canto itself, then going through the Hollander notes while reading the Canto a second time. There was ritual aspect to it. Then I finished and had nothing I felt I needed to say. This is and was so strange it's stumped my ability to write anything at all about this. So below is more a report than a review. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595113792i/29838316._SX540_.jpg" width="1000" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>Purgatorio</i> is nothing like <i>Inferno</i> in its impact. Here nothing is permanent, and the tragic aspects are not only a whole lot milder, but also are subsumed by Dante's own purging and entering into Garden of Eden. Dante can and does create and build on the sense of adventure, fascination, or narrative space and dimensions he created in <i>Inferno</i>, but he couldn't possibly duplicate the wonderful awfulness of his first book, and yet <i>Inferno</i> leaves its lingering presence here. Its enough.<br><br>Like he did with Hell, Dante here definitively defined the idea of Purgatory for the rest of history. Even more so here, as he had no artwork or narrative to work with. Purgatory was only defined by the Catholic church in 1274, at the Second Council of Lyon, a place in the afterlife for purging of sinful but uncondemned lives, especially for those who came back to Christ at the last moments before death, and after an otherwise sinful life. It's a loose idea to work with. Unlike Heaven and Hell, Purgatory has a time element. Souls pass pass through. And they have a goal, a purpose to weather whatever challenges this world throws at them. <br><br>Dante creates an ante-purgatory, and entry place for the souls on the right side of the razors edge, but who still haven't managed entry into Purgatory proper. They are welcomed with a surprise, the Roman Cato, a hero of the lost Republic. His cohorts, Brutus and Cassius, were especially selected, along with Judas Iscariot, for the most prominent position in the lowest level of hell, in the mouth of Lucifer, eternally gnawed on. So it's a mystery as to why Cato has so much better an outcome. I think its, in a way, a kind of statement by Dante that he's in charge of this world and it follows his rules. The second surprise of a sort is that Virgil has to figure out where to go. Dante's fearless guide in <i>Inferno</i>, who had travelled and knew the whole length of that world, even though he resided in the relatively pleasant Limbo space, has never made it this far. He's out of his element, and has to find his way, and he is worse off then everyone around him. Purgatory is a place of hope, of sustained pain accepted, even embraced, as the price of entry into Heaven. Virgil, condemned to Limbo, has no such hope.<br><br>Once in Purgatory, our pair wander through the seven sins (Pride, Envy, Wrath, Sloth, Avarice, Gluttony and Lust), each a layer with its own entry, and messages that are either visual or made of voices that just come out of the air, its own purging punishments appropriate for that sin, and each with an exit. They will meet famous characters with mixed records, and deceased and flawed one-time associates from Dante's life, and they will deal with their own challenges, exhaustions, uncertainties and visions. They will even meet one who has completed the process and is now saved, the Roman Poet Statius, author of the <i>Thebiad</i> and the unfinished epic, the <i>Achilleida</i>. Statius was devoted to Virgil's work, and saw him as the great poet. Unlike Virgil, Statius lived long enough to learn of Christ. Dante allows him a secret late saving conversion. <br><br>Statius hangs around and the three poets enter the Garden of Eden together, the earthly heaven. They are met first by a mysterious beauty, Matelda, then Beatrice herself, Dante's guiding light, and finally a heavenly procession with symbolic virtues, a Christ-like griffin and so on. What takes place is play of Dante with love and sex (and knowledge) contrasted with the ideal and purified sexless divine experience. Dante will characterize these non-sexual ladies with sexually charged poetic references, and go through a series of marriage-references with his Beatrice. He tells us, "<i>desire upon desire so seized me</i>.” It's a playful fight with Dante learning to gain control over his own will. Virgil will bless him as master of his will, a success, and then Virgil will fade away. Beatrice shrugs off the loss of our guide, merely commenting, famously, "<i>Dante, because Virgil has departed</i>”, and carries on. That line is considered the climax of the whole Comedy and of this book. <br><br>Dante, of course, is not done. He will be led by Matelda through the river Lethe, forgetting his sins, and be rewarded, especially, with the famous smile of Beatrice. <i>"And then I shared the temporary blindness of those whose eyes have just been smitten by the sun, leaving me sightless for a time."</i> Beatrice is not a bride, but more of a Christ-like figure, or maybe a Christ-bride. She gives Dante a prophecy and an order to record his experience. Finally Matelda leads him though the second river in the garden, the River Eunoe. This is Dante's own creation, and the river strengthens his memories of his good deeds. And so it ends.<br><br>-----------------------------------------------<br><br>35. <b>Purgatorio by Dante Alighieri</b><br><b>translation and notes</b>: Jean Hollander &amp; Robert Hollander<br><b>published</b>: 1320, translation 2003<br><b>format</b>: 827-page Paperback, with original Italian, translation and notes<br><b>acquired</b>: September<br><b>read</b>: May 3 – Jun 28<br><b>time reading</b>: 38 hr 6 min, 2.8 min/page<br><b>rating</b>: 5<br><b>locations</b>: Purgatory (antipodal to Jerusalem)<br><b>about the author</b> Florentine poet, c. 1265 – 1321</span>
  <a data-text-id="14598282198733567901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_176693820" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176693820" />
    <a title="Justin Evans" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2184529-justin-evans"><img alt="Justin Evans" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319658063p2/2184529.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176693820?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Justin Evans" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Justin Evans" href="/user/show/2184529-justin-evans">Justin Evans</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385497008" href="/book/show/208472.Purgatorio">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2184529-justin-evans?shelf=poetry-and-drama">poetry-and-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176693820" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10865514089313997032">There are two kinds of people who read Dante. The first kind gets all excited about people stuck head down in piles of shit, and wishes that the adulterers and libertines could just keep on doing what they did in the real world, because it's so romantic. The second kind gets all excited about griffins pulling chariots, the relationship between the political and the religious, and the neoplatonic ascent from beautiful woman to Beauty and God. I am the second kind; I can see the pull of the first </span>
  <span id="freeText10865514089313997032" style="display:none">There are two kinds of people who read Dante. The first kind gets all excited about people stuck head down in piles of shit, and wishes that the adulterers and libertines could just keep on doing what they did in the real world, because it's so romantic. The second kind gets all excited about griffins pulling chariots, the relationship between the political and the religious, and the neoplatonic ascent from beautiful woman to Beauty and God. I am the second kind; I can see the pull of the first kind, and I understand it, but really. The whole thing just gets better the further on it goes. Hell is like a decent TV drama with an episode each week, say, House. Purgatorio (and, from memory, Paradiso) are to Inferno what The Wire is to House. Sometimes you just want to watch 45 minutes of cool stuff; sometimes you want something a bit less immediately gratifying, but a more substantial. And this is the substance. <br /><br />Luckily, the Hollanders are here to translate this thing for you and to give you the insider knowledge you'll need to get a hold of that substance. It isn't easy, unless you're a medievalist who knows the psalms by heart in latin, which I am not and, I'm guessing, neither are you. Because those people are not writing or reading goodreads reviews. They are studying ancient manuscripts and debating whether that letter there is an iota or a lambda. Good for them. Good for the head in a bucket of shit loving people. Good for all. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10865514089313997032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Inferno - yes. Paradise - yes. That middle part? Their theory seems to be Heaven or Hell, with no middle ground.<br /><br />So I made it my December book purchase. Having waited two weeks to <i>get</i> to December and then another week for the used copy to get to me ... I am now extremely happy to have this lovely Modern Library edition in my hands. In many ways my wait to get the book is symbolic of the souls in Purgatory, willing to suffer to achieve ultimate good. I now embark upon my pilgrimage with Dante to turn that crooked road into a straight road by beginning the trip around that mountain. <br /><br />-----------<br /><br />As with the previous time I read Purgatorio, I really loved this book and found it personally inspiring. i was so moved by the very end that I even shed a few tears of joy.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer681520829" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17333929631500842911">This is my favorite canticle of the Divina Commedia. Sure, it's not as thrilling or fascinatingly grotesque (/grotesquely fascinating) as the Inferno, but the literary images are breathtakingly beautiful, not to mention extremely powerful. As a modern reader, it's hard not to be moved by Dante's poetry, even 7 centuries later, a fact that attests to the immortality of the work. Those who are unfamiliar with Dante and/or Italian Literature and stop reading the Commedia after the Inferno are missi</span>
  <span id="freeText17333929631500842911" style="display:none">This is my favorite canticle of the Divina Commedia. Sure, it's not as thrilling or fascinatingly grotesque (/grotesquely fascinating) as the Inferno, but the literary images are breathtakingly beautiful, not to mention extremely powerful. As a modern reader, it's hard not to be moved by Dante's poetry, even 7 centuries later, a fact that attests to the immortality of the work. Those who are unfamiliar with Dante and/or Italian Literature and stop reading the Commedia after the Inferno are missing out big time! I personally think the Paradiso is relatively boring, but for the more pious readers it can also be heart-stopping at times. <br /><br />My favorite part of the Purgatorio is canti 29-31 (and slightly beyond), wherein Dante glimpses his beloved for the first time in decades on the banks of the river Lethe. As she unveils her face, her organic beauty is so radiant that the pilgrim is nearly blinded by her, and he weeps as she scolds him for straying in his devotion to her after her passing. Her arrival is accompanied by a magnificent triumphal procession, meant to symbolize the Church; some scholars argue that Beatrice also serves the same function. The triumphal chariot is pulled by a gryphon - obviously a symbol of Jesus Christ.<br /><br />I'm currently working toward my doctoral degree in Early Modern (Medieval/Renaissance) Italian Literature, and it seems as though every time I take a course on Dante or lyric poetry or even Boccaccio and Petrarch (the other two "Crowns" that make up the famous "tre corone" of the Italian canon), I can't help but write about some part or another of the Purgatorio. Whether it's Ulysse's prideful, unnecessary death, or Beatrice's unveiling, or the triumphal chariots as a motif in the literature of the time period, I find myself coming back to the strangely familiar words of Dante like a comfort food. Dante mentions - and I can't remember exactly where just now - that even some of the worst sinners in the hour, minute, second of their death need only murmur even the name of Mary to be considered for admission into Purgatory (I'm getting this wrong, probably. Comps are coming up soon and I'll get it down pat eventually.) If you choose to read the Commedia in order - as you should - the Purgatorio is quite refreshing after making it through Inferno, yet the messages are still stern enough to get the reader to pay mind to his every action in this life. I'd be lying if I said some of the episodes, antiquated now as they may be, didn't affect some of my own personal decisions or renew the beautiful aspects of my Catholic upbringing.<br /><br />I also highly recommend this edition of the text (by Durling and Martinez). The whole Commedia is rife with allegorical references, so a good edition is key to understanding it fully and maximizing your Dante experience!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17333929631500842911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer303405461974609923">I'm apparently reading the Divine Comedy backwards this time, since I finished the Paradiso on Dec 31, and then read this one for our church faith exploration book club. This is probably the edition I would recommend to anybody trying the Comedy--the notes are thorough (maybe too thorough) and usually to the point. Hollander does not suffer fools gladly, though, and his dismissal of other scholars can be a bit abrupt. The translation is sometimes quite wonderful, and as far as I can tell, accura</span>
  <span id="freeText303405461974609923" style="display:none">I'm apparently reading the Divine Comedy backwards this time, since I finished the Paradiso on Dec 31, and then read this one for our church faith exploration book club. This is probably the edition I would recommend to anybody trying the Comedy--the notes are thorough (maybe too thorough) and usually to the point. Hollander does not suffer fools gladly, though, and his dismissal of other scholars can be a bit abrupt. The translation is sometimes quite wonderful, and as far as I can tell, accurate. This is the first time I actually felt some of the responses that a reader of Italian is supposed to feel--the immense relief of cool, fresh, bright openness in the first canto, after the horrible darkness, pressure, and claustrophobia of the Inferno. The huge surprise when Dante finally actually meets his beloved Beatrice in the Earthly Paradise, and finds her not at all what he (or the reader) expected. Very interesting to read this while listening to <u>Acedia and Me</u>, since the mountain of Purgatory is all about washing away the effects of the seven deadly sins. Kathleen Norris' references to Dante are fairly good, but her understanding of acedia is not at all what his was.</span>
  <a data-text-id="303405461974609923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6162436027973562460" style="display:none">If Dante knew how hard it would be for modern readers to interpret his Commedia, he would have invoked a beautitude of Jesus sung by the angels just for us...."blessed are those who persevere reading Purgatorio...." <br /><br /><br /><br />Purgatorio is way more complex and interesting than the Inferno - but it requires a lot more effort to read it. <div id="review_1578686547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1578686547" />
    <a title="Ashley Adams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21959354-ashley-adams"><img alt="Ashley Adams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454082818p2/21959354.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1578686547?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley Adams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley Adams" href="/user/show/21959354-ashley-adams">Ashley Adams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780195004137" href="/book/show/150026.The_Divine_Comedy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=dante">dante</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=ts-eliot">ts-eliot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=frankenstein">frankenstein</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=italian-studies">italian-studies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=group-read">group-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=cuc">cuc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21959354-ashley-adams?shelf=cwc">cwc</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1578686547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5599152776281618143">I thoroughly enjoyed Dante's journey into the land of redemption. The imagery is beautiful and story line engaging. The Hollander and Hollander translation provides helpful summaries to the content of each canto. Also, the Hollanders' notes synthesize hundreds of years of Dantean criticism- fabulous scholarship! The only thing missing would be lost in translation anyway, so the Hollanders supply Italian and English translation side by side. This edition may seem an insurmountable tome, but it is</span>
  <span id="freeText5599152776281618143" style="display:none">I thoroughly enjoyed Dante's journey into the land of redemption. The imagery is beautiful and story line engaging. The Hollander and Hollander translation provides helpful summaries to the content of each canto. Also, the Hollanders' notes synthesize hundreds of years of Dantean criticism- fabulous scholarship! The only thing missing would be lost in translation anyway, so the Hollanders supply Italian and English translation side by side. This edition may seem an insurmountable tome, but it is possible (and rewarding) to reach the top of this mountain.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_299081251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/299081251" />
    <a title="Zach Pickens" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1329437-zach-pickens"><img alt="Zach Pickens" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/299081251?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zach Pickens" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zach Pickens" href="/user/show/1329437-zach-pickens">Zach Pickens</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer299081251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7155631321723774633">After trekking through the depths of hell, Dante, led by his guide and literary father Virgil, ascends the seven storied mountain of purgatory. Like hell, the souls’ torments fit the sins that engaged them in life, but unlike hell, the shades are not so much punished as they are purging themselves of the capital sins. Virgil and Dante reach the pinnacle of the mountain, the Garden of Eden, where Virgil departs leaving Dante with his next guide, Dante’s great love, Beatrice.<br/>A few thoughts:<br/><b>Virgil </b></span>
  <span id="freeText7155631321723774633" style="display:none">After trekking through the depths of hell, Dante, led by his guide and literary father Virgil, ascends the seven storied mountain of purgatory. Like hell, the souls’ torments fit the sins that engaged them in life, but unlike hell, the shades are not so much punished as they are purging themselves of the capital sins. Virgil and Dante reach the pinnacle of the mountain, the Garden of Eden, where Virgil departs leaving Dante with his next guide, Dante’s great love, Beatrice.<br />A few thoughts:<br /><b>Virgil in hell</b><br />What seems unjust to many commentators is Dante’s placement of Virgil in hell, especially notable because Dante believes that Virgil’s poetry was wise enough to contain a spark or the spirit of Christianity even before Christ. For example, the poet Statius credits Virgil’s writing with his turn to Christianity. According to the wonderful website DanteWorlds published by the University of Texas at Austin, Dante was in a tradition of medieval scholars who believed that Virgil was a forefather of Christianity—Virgil is writing before Christ’s birth, but his work is incorporated into the literary genealogy of Christian Rome. There are even a few pre-Christian pagans existing outside of hell—one in purgatory, two in paradise, but even at the end of the poem, it is clear that Virgil will not be joining them. <br /><br />Virgil does not argue against the seemingly unfair set of rules that keeps him in limbo. At one point in hell, when Dante is weeping over the horrible punishments, Virgil chastises him saying (I’m paraphrasing) Hey, this is all just part of God’s divine justice—everything is as it should be according to the supreme Good, so stop crying and grow up. I wonder if this is how Virgil sees his own fate. I wonder if there is wisdom in his acceptance of the divine, immutable law that keeps him in hell—acceptance as opposed to fighting what cannot be fought. Why argue? It’d be like disagreeing with the law of gravity. <br /><br />He also might be a victim of his allegorical status as “Reason.” Raymond Schoder, in an older article in The Classical Journal, defends Dante’s placement of Virgil in hell because, allegorically, the poem wouldn’t work otherwise; there is a place where “Reason” alone can go no further and where faith and love complete us. In Dante’s epic poem, this place is the Garden of Eden, where Virgil is replaced with Beatrice. <br /><br /><b>Virgil’s humanity</b><br />Virgil is dutiful (a characteristic prized by Rome and exemplified by Virgil’s Aeneas). But it is his dutifulness mixed with the rigidity of Dante’s universe that also gives Virgil’s character the sense of someone subverted; but this makes sense, not only because it is Dante’s journey, not Virgil’s, but also because Limbo is still a place without hope, and wisdom without hope still makes those shades languid. In Limbo, where Virgil abides, there is a “meadow of fresh verdure” where the greatest of the shades live. But the shades speak softly, and move slowly. They are forever left with desire unfulfilled; they are without hope. <br /><br />For me, this hopelessness is poignantly at odds with the humanity Virgil displays. Canto I ends with a beautiful exchange between Virgil and Dante: as the sun rises over the plain where they are walking, Virgil bends down and softly places his hands in the dewy grass and then uses that water to clean the tears from Dante’s face. Allegorically, this is “Reason” washing away the sins in preparation for the journey of purgation. But at the literal surface, the gesture and the physical sensation of it is touching. This is a poet who has spent over thirteen hundred years in a place without hope, without the earthly sun, without the morning dew. He must remember what it felt like to have the sun warm his face, the sound of the ocean at his side, the feel of the fresh grass beneath his feet. True, limbo isn’t as terrible as the other circles of hell—Virgil stays with the other poets in a place “open, luminous, and high,” but I imagine this does not compare with the nature of the living world. <br /><br />Like most people, I know the pleasant sensation of placing my hands in the cool dew that has collected on the blades of grass, of letting my hands brush against the clean blades. It feels cleansing. And so when Virgil places his hands in the cool wet grass in the morning sun, I wonder if he feels any hope contained in that nature. I wonder if he can keep some of that dew for himself. As someone who has been so long under the earth, as a poet who is able to take the sensations of the world and use them to express some greater meaning, how can this fail to touch him? The sun is rising. It is Easter, a time for resurrection and renewal. To wash the tears of someone’s face is an act of kindness--even love--that must come from a place very human, despite the abridgment of humanity in Limbo. To complete this gesture—to give comfort like this—is moving.<br /><br /><b>Beatrice in Canto XXX</b><br />After the divine instruction along the ascent to the top of purgatory, the appearance of Beatrice allegorically marks the transition from Reason to the Divine love and faith that accompanies true understanding. Outside of allegory, the experience of meeting Beatrice melts the ice around his heart, leaving him without any protection. Beatrice rebukes him—she is “like an admiral….” Noticing that Virgil has vanished, Dante starts to weep and Beatrice tells him to cease weeping for Virgil’s absence, for “it behooves thee to weep by another sword.” Loosely—I’ll give you something to cry about. <br /><br />I found this initial interaction very powerful. If this was a movie, the set would dim and go silent. This is not a pleasant reuniting; she lays out his sins frankly, with stern pity. Purgatory, indeed heaven and hell are all places where one cannot hide, cannot lie, or pretend. Every fact, deed, and thought of the living get sorted out in the uncompromising Truth of the afterlife—one’s sins are on display, which is shameful—Dante cannot stand to see his reflection in the water while Beatrice speaks to him. I think it’s fitting that this occurs in the Garden of Eden because he is essentially denuded, forced to see himself as he really is. Dante must truly walk through the fire, must face his sins—his turning away from her, from salvation. <br /><br />Perhaps because this scene comes after so much climbing--so much instruction with example of example of sin and repentance, it has the effect of a powerful transition, an emotional break with everything that has come before it. Dante has spent sixty three Cantos dutifully learning with Virgil, but he is utterly broken after a few words with his love Beatrice. <br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7155631321723774633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <td colspan="2">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1189579547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1189579547" />
    <a title="Vanessa J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j"><img alt="Vanessa J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468102148p2/19590155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1189579547?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa J." href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1189579547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6580234501372969197">My journey continues with Purgatorio. Now that I've finally gotten out of Hell, I come to the place where all the sinners who repented before they died are.<br/><br/>Before knowing where I am, I feel uneasiness. "I'm lost", I thought. Gratefully, my sorrow does not last longer, for I found my Guide. We are in Purgatorio. As its name says, and as I said before, this is the place for paying off mischiefs done. I somehow fear it, because, you know, I am no saint!<br/><br/>Okay, enough poetry. Now I'll tell you how Pur</span>
  <span id="freeText6580234501372969197" style="display:none">My journey continues with Purgatorio. Now that I've finally gotten out of Hell, I come to the place where all the sinners who repented before they died are.<br /><br />Before knowing where I am, I feel uneasiness. "I'm lost", I thought. Gratefully, my sorrow does not last longer, for I found my Guide. We are in Purgatorio. As its name says, and as I said before, this is the place for paying off mischiefs done. I somehow fear it, because, you know, I am no saint!<br /><br />Okay, enough poetry. Now I'll tell you how Purgatorio is built...<br /><br />Just as Hell, it is divided in different stages, each one for a different kind of sin. This time, the lighter the deeds are paid off in the higher parts of the mountain; and the worst ones are in the lower parts of the mount.<br /><br />These are the stairs in which Purgatorio is organised (each one corresponds to one of the capital sins):<br /><br />1. <b>Pride:</b> I really pray Sherlock Holmes felt guilty of his arrogance at some point in his life. And I really, <i>really</i> pray Pellinore Warthrop did the same too, although I really doubt it. If you died proud, then I guess I'll see you in Hell.<br />2. <b>Envy:</b> You should be content with what you have. It's not good to desire other people's things. Just kidding. We all envy something from someone. But it does no harm to be happy with our own possessions.<br />3. <b>Wrath:</b> Uh-oh. I get angry pretty easily, since my level of patience is very limited. I should be careful the next time it happens. The good thing is that I always feel bad after my anger vanishes.<br />4. <b>Sloth:</b> Oh, God, how I hate this sin! I <i>hate</i> lazy people. It might be because in the laboratories I have only worked with these kind of people...and because in group projects, somehow, the lazy always end up with me. I wouldn't fogive them, if it was mine the decision to make.<br />5. <b>Greed:</b> Is greed for books a sin? And if so, does it have to be paid in here? Because if it is so, I don't think I would ever repent from wanting more and more books.<br />6. <b>Gluttony:</b> This one wouldn't be a problem for me. Believe it or not, for me, food is not such a great pleasure as people acclaim for it to be. So, if you're a fan of gorging with no end, then...you'd better stop that.<br />7. <b>Lust:</b> Oh, my...didn't I tell you that there are more things in life than sex? At least you realised that before it was too late.<br /><br />I had a problem with this book. Or rather I have a problem with <i>poetry</i>. For some unknown reason, I found this book harder to understand. Maybe it is because I've been recently reading a lot of books with a poetical writing and my brain is already burnt from so many struggle in trying to figure out what is happening...or this one was more complicated.<br /><br />Both <i>Inferno</i> and <i>Purgatorio</i> were good, but which one is better? I know not. I think I liked <i>Inferno</i> more. I don't know how much that fact tells from me, but the story in <i>Inferno</i> was more interesting. And – let's face it —the bad things are the ones who call the most our attention.<br /><br />Now I'm off to Heaven! Wait for me, God! You will be pleased to meet me! (I know I had promised in my review about <i>Inferno</i> that I would leave this kind of jokes aside, but I couldn't help it: This book is given for that.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6580234501372969197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('v2sxhXtMsFIIGDnFmK186IGtILYNFTfWsG/dA04WFeE/BNbZ+3Obauc05LDRPlh59mJLjChN+WIHV/kvg/UoRA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TiijaOqF2HeBOHTDOlALO8WLlmzvG7DmgngRhPLW/GnOR0Q0arrzT24UqbZzwy+qskT9VspDflI1QDWoPzXBzA==')})
  }

Assieme alle Confessioni di un italiano di Ippolito Nievo è considerato il più importante della letteratura italiana prima dell\'unità nazionale. Fu pubblicato in una prima versione nel 1827; rivisto in seguito dallo stesso autore, soprattutto nel linguaggio, fu ripubblicato nella versione definitiva fra il 1840 e il 1841.\nAmbientato dal 1628 al 1630 in Lombardia durante l\'occupazione spagnola, fu il primo esempio di romanzo storico della letteratura italiana. Secondo un\'interpretazione risorgimentista, il periodo storico era stato scelto da Manzoni con l\'intento di alludere al dominio austriaco sul nord Italia. Quella che Manzoni vuole descrivere è la società italiana di ogni tempo, con tutti i suoi difetti che tuttora mantiene.[1] Il romanzo si basa su una rigorosa ricerca storica e gli episodi del XVII secolo, come ad esempio le vicende della Monaca di Monza e la grande peste del 1629-1631, si fondano tutti su documenti d\'archivio e cronache dell\'epoca.\nIl romanzo di Manzoni viene considerato non solo una pietra miliare della letteratura italiana, ma anche un passaggio fondamentale nella nascita stessa della lingua italiana. Nei dialoghi, riporta anche diversi esempi di parlato spontaneo non ammissibili nella lingua standard.
